\begin{itemize}
    \item If $n$ is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real number $x_n$ such that
\[

\[

\]
\end{itemize}
If a player cannot choose a suitable $x_n$, the game ends and the other player wins. If the game goes on forever, neither player wins. All chosen numbers are known to both players.
Determine all values of $\lambda$ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

We need to analyze the game in terms of cumulative sums and squares. Let's denote the state after each turn. Also, the constraints on the moves themselves: For Alice, she chooses x_m such that S_m = S_{m-1} + x_m ≤ λ m. So she will choose x_m to try to increase squares. To determine if Bazza can force a win for λ=0.9, we need to analyze the game systematically.

Our earlier analysis suggests that for λ > √2, Alice can win immediately. But we saw that for λ=1, she can win on turn 3. So for λ=0.9, we need to see if Alice can still win. Actually we need to determine the winning region for Alice and Bazza. The problem asks: Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy. So we need to find the set of λ for which Alice can force a win, and the set for which Bazza can force a win. Possibly there is a range where neither has a winning strategy (draw). Let's analyze the game mathematically.

Define the game state after each turn. The game is about two cumulative constraints. Let's denote the sequences: x_1, x_2, ... nonnegative reals.

Constraints:

For odd n: S_n ≤ λ n.
For even n: Q_n ≤ n.

Game ends when a player cannot make a move. So Alice loses on her turn if S_{n-1} > λ n (since she cannot choose x_n to make S_n ≤ λ n, even x_n=0 gives S_n = S_{n-1} > λ n). Similarly, Bazza loses on his turn if Q_{n-1} > n.

So the game is about controlling the sums.

We can think of the game as: Alice wants to eventually make Q_{odd} > odd+1 (so Bazza loses on next turn). Bazza wants to eventually make S_{even} > λ (even+1) (so Alice loses on her next turn). But note that the constraints are cumulative, so it's about the sequences.

Idea: The game is similar to a "pursuit" where each player tries to push the other's resource over a threshold. There is a known technique: consider the "potential" function F_n = S_n - λ n (for odd n) and G_n = Q_n - n (for even n). The game ends when either potential becomes positive (for the player who is about to move). The players can choose numbers to affect these potentials.

We need to find for which λ there is a strategy for Alice to force that for some odd m, Q_m > m+1, i.e., Bazza cannot move. Conversely, for which λ Bazza can force that for some even n, S_n > λ n+? Actually if Bazza makes S_{even} > λ (even+1), then Alice cannot move on her next turn (since S_{even} > λ (even+1)). But careful: Alice's turn after even n is at odd n+1. She needs S_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+1). She can choose x_{n+1} such that S_{n+1} = S_n + x_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+1). If S_n > λ (n+1), then even with x_{n+1}=0, S_{n+1}=S_n > λ (n+1), so she loses. So condition for Bazza to win is that for some even n, S_n > λ (n+1). Since n is even, n+1 is odd. So Bazza wants to make S_{even} > λ (even+1). Similarly, Alice wants to make Q_{odd} > odd+1.

So we need to analyze the game in terms of these "excess" functions.

Let's define:

For odd m: Let E_m = Q_m - (m+1). Alice wins if she can make E_m > 0 for some odd m.
For even n: Let F_n = S_n - λ (n+1). Bazza wins if he can make F_n > 0 for some even n.

But note that the constraints for moves also involve these potentials. For Alice's turn at odd m, she must ensure that S_m ≤ λ m (so she doesn't lose immediately). She can choose x_m to try to increase Q_m. Similarly, Bazza's turn at even n must ensure Q_n ≤ n, and he can choose x_n to try to increase S_n.

So the game is about two resources: sum S and sum of squares Q. The players have opposite goals.

We can try to find a "safe" region where both can continue indefinitely, and determine the threshold for which one player can force a win.

Observation: The game is symmetric in a sense if we transform variables. Consider the "dual" game: replace λ with 1/λ? Not exactly.

Let's attempt to find a critical λ by considering the "ideal" play where both players try to keep the game going but also try to force a win. Often in such games, there is a threshold λ0 such that if λ > λ0, Alice can win; if λ < λ0, Bazza can win; and at λ = λ0, the game can be infinite (draw). We need to find λ0.

From earlier, we saw that for λ > √2 ≈ 1.4142, Alice can win immediately on her first move by playing x_1 = √2 (which is ≤ λ). But for λ ≤ √2, she cannot win on first move. For λ = 1.4 (<√2), can she win later? Possibly there is a threshold around 1? Let's test λ=1.2. Can Alice win? She wants to eventually make Q_odd > odd+1. Since λ is 1.2, she can choose x_1 up to 1.2. She might try to build up squares. But Bazza can also try to make S_even > λ (even+1). For λ=1.2, λ (even+1) is about 1.2*(2k+1) = 2.4k+1.2. So if Bazza can push S_{2k} above 2.4k+1.2, then Alice loses. This seems plausible.

We need a more systematic approach.

Define the "resource" constraints:

After each odd turn, the sum S_n is constrained: S_n ≤ λ n. After each even turn, the sum of squares Q_n is constrained: Q_n ≤ n.

But note that these constraints are not independent: by Cauchy-Schwarz, S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. So if Q_n is small, S_n is also small.

We can consider the "maximal" possible S_n given Q_n, and vice versa.

We can try to see if there is a strategy for Bazza to always keep S_n ≤ λ n for all odd n, and also keep Q_n ≤ n for all even n, and prevent Alice from ever making Q_odd > odd+1. That would be a draw. But we need to find λ for which Bazza can force a win.

Let's try to find a strategy for Bazza to make S_n exceed λ (n+1) for some even n.

Bazza can choose x_n on his turns to increase S_n. But he must also respect Q_n ≤ n. So he can allocate square budget to increase sum.

We can think of the game as: Alice chooses x_1, then Bazza chooses x_2, then Alice chooses x_3, etc. They have perfect information.

We can try to compute the "critical" λ by considering the worst-case for Alice: if she wants to avoid losing, she will try to keep S_n as small as possible to prevent Bazza from exceeding λ (n+1). She can also try to build up squares to eventually win.

Alternatively, we can treat the game as a two-player game with a "potential" function that is monotone and see if one player can force a win by using a "strategy stealing" argument.

I think the problem is known: Inekoalaty game. I recall a problem from an IMO Shortlist or something similar: "Alice and Bazza play the inekoalaty game, a two-player game whose rules depend on a positive real number λ. On the nth turn, if n is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real x_n such that x_1+...+x_n ≤ λ n. If n is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real x_n such that x_1^2+...+x_n^2 ≤ n. If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the game ends and the other player wins. Determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all for which Bazza has a winning strategy." Possibly the answer: Alice wins if λ > 1, Bazza wins if λ < 1, and if λ = 1, the game is a draw (both can survive indefinitely). But we saw that for λ=1, Alice can win on turn 3? Let's consider the "resource" trade-off. For a given sum S_n, the minimal sum of squares is achieved by equal numbers. Conversely, for a given sum of squares Q_n, the maximal sum is achieved by concentrating mass. So if Alice wants to increase squares, she can use a large x_m, but that uses sum budget. The sum budget is λ n. So she cannot exceed λ n. So the maximum possible Q_m after her move is when she makes x_m as large as possible, but then the sum might be close to λ m. However, she also has to respect that the sum before her move is S_{m-1}. So she can choose x_m up to λ m - S_{m-1}. Then Q_m = Q_{m-1} + x_m^2. So the maximum possible Q_m given previous state is Q_{m-1} + (λ m - S_{m-1})^2. She wants this to exceed m+1.

Bazza, on his turns, can choose x_n to increase S_n. He can allocate square budget to increase sum. The maximum sum he can achieve given Q_{n-1} is S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). Because he can choose x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) to maximize sum. So the maximum possible S_n after his move is S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}).

Thus the game can be seen as: at each turn, the player can choose a value that affects the state. The player can choose any value between 0 and the maximum allowed. To force a win, they can try to make the potential exceed the threshold.

We need to find if there is a strategy for Alice to make Q_{odd} > odd+1. Similarly, Bazza wants to make S_{even} > λ (even+1).

Consider the "optimal" play: Alice will try to build up squares, while Bazza will try to build up sum. They have opposite goals. The game is deterministic if both play optimally to win.

We can try to compute the "critical" λ by considering the "worst-case" for Alice: if she tries to maximize Q_m, she must minimize the sum S_{m-1} to have room for x_m. She wants to increase squares, so she wants x_m large, but that uses sum budget. So there is a trade-off: she can use a large x_m to increase squares, but then the sum might be high, which could allow Bazza to increase sum on his next turn. However, she can also try to keep the sum low to prevent Bazza from exceeding λ (n+1). So she needs to find a balance.

So for λ=0.9, Bazza can force a win by playing x_2 = 1.3 and then always using the maximum allowed sum increase on his turns. The first move x_1=0.5 worked, but maybe Alice can choose a different x_1 to avoid the loss? In the simulation, we used x_1=0.5 and then on odd turns we used the maximum allowed (x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}) which is the greedy strategy. That led to a win for Bazza. But maybe if she chooses a different x_1, she could still win? Let's analyze the game more generally.

We need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy. The above suggests that for λ=0.9, Bazza wins. For λ=1, Alice wins. So there is a critical λ0 between 0.9 and 1. Possibly λ0 = 1? But we saw that for λ=1, Alice can win. So the threshold must be ≤1. Let's test λ=0.95. Is Alice able to win? Let's try the greedy strategy: choose x_1=0.5 (to keep sum low? So for λ=0.98, Bazza wins. For λ=0.99, Alice wins. So the threshold λ0 is between 0.98 and 0.99. Possibly λ0 = 0.99? But we need to determine the exact value. Let's try to analyze the recurrence more systematically. The greedy strategy for Alice: on odd turns, she plays x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (the maximum allowed to keep S_n ≤ λ n). This is a natural strategy to maximize her square increase. For Bazza, he plays x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) on even turns to maximize sum. This is also natural. So the state evolves deterministically: for n odd, S_n = λ n, and Q_n = Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. For n even, S_n = S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}), and Q_n = Q_{n-1} + (sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}))^2 = Q_{n-1} + (n - Q_{n-1}) = n. So after each even turn, Q_n = n exactly. So the sequence of states is: after each even turn, Q_n = n; after each odd turn, S_n = λ n.

So we can compute the state after each pair of turns. Let's define the state at the start of each odd turn (i.e., after an even turn). Let m be odd. After even turn n = m-1, we have Q_{m-1} = m-1 (since m-1 is even). And S_{m-1} is some value. Then Alice's move at m: she chooses x_m = λ m - S_{m-1}, so S_m = λ m. Then Q_m = Q_{m-1} + (λ m - S_{m-1})^2 = (m-1) + (λ m - S_{m-1})^2. Then Bazza's move at m+1 (even): he chooses x_{m+1} = sqrt((m+1) - Q_m) ) = sqrt( (m+1) - (m-1 + (λ m - S_{m-1})^2) ) = sqrt( 2 - (λ m - S_{m-1})^2 ). Then S_{m+1} = S_m + sqrt((m+1) - Q_m) = λ m + sqrt(2 - (λ m - S_{m-1})^2). And Q_{m+1} = m+1 (since after even turn, Q equals the index). So the state at the start of the next odd turn (m+2) is: Q_{m+1} = m+1, and S_{m+1} = λ m + sqrt(2 - (λ m - S_{m-1})^2). So we have a recurrence for S_{odd} values. Let’s denote a_k = S_{2k+1} for k ≥ 0? Actually we need to be consistent: Let m = 2k+1 (odd). Then after even turn 2k, we have Q_{2k} = 2k, and S_{2k} = some value. Then Alice's move at 2k+1: S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1). Then Q_{2k+1} = 2k + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2. Then Bazza's move at 2k+2: x_{2k+2} = sqrt( (2k+2) - Q_{2k+1} ) = sqrt( (2k+2) - (2k + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2 ) = sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2. Then S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2) = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2). And Q_{2k+2} = 2k+2. So the recurrence for S_{2k} (even) is: S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2). This is a discrete dynamical system. The initial S_0 = 0. Then we compute S_2 = λ*1 + sqrt(2 - (λ*1 - 0)^2) = λ + sqrt(2 - λ^2). Then S_4 = λ*3 + sqrt(2 - (λ*3 - S_2)^2), etc. This recurrence determines the sum after each even turn.

Alice wins if for some odd m, Q_m > m+1, i.e., if (m-1) + (λ m - S_{m-1})^2 > m+1, i.e., (λ m - S_{m-1})^2 > 2, i.e., λ m - S_{m-1} > √2. So the condition for Alice to win on her turn at odd m is that λ m - S_{m-1} > √2. Note that this is the amount she adds to her sum on that turn. If she can make this > √2, then the square sum after her move exceeds m+1, so Bazza loses on the next even turn. So Alice's goal is to eventually have the sum before her turn, S_{m-1}, be such that the gap λ m - S_{m-1} > √2. Since λ m is the allowed sum for her move, she wants S_{m-1} to be sufficiently small relative to λ m.

Bazza's goal is to make S_{m-1} large enough so that λ m - S_{m-1} ≤ √2 for all m (i.e., Alice never gets a gap > √2). But if he can make S_{m-1} > λ m - √2, then the gap is ≤ √2. Actually if S_{m-1} > λ m - √2, then λ m - S_{m-1} < √2, so Alice cannot win on that turn. So Bazza wants to keep S_{m-1} > λ m - √2 for all odd m. Since S_{m-1} is after an even turn, and he controls the even turns, he can try to increase S_{m-1} enough.

Now note that the recurrence for S_{2k} (even) is: S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2). Let’s denote g(x) = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - x)^2). Then S_{2k+2} = g(S_{2k}). So we have a sequence starting from S_0 = 0. The fixed point of this recurrence (if it converges) would be a value x such that x = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - x)^2). But note that the recurrence depends on k, so it's not autonomous. However, we can consider the transformation. Let’s define a new variable: y_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}. Then the recurrence becomes: S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - y_k^2). Then y_{k+1} = λ(2k+3) - S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+3) - (λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - y_k^2)) = 2λ + sqrt(2 - y_k^2) - (something)? Actually λ(2k+3) - λ(2k+1) = 2λ. So y_{k+1} = 2λ - sqrt(2 - y_k^2). So we have a recurrence for y_k: y_{k+1} = 2λ - sqrt(2 - y_k^2). This is an autonomous recurrence! And the initial y_0 = λ*1 - S_0 = λ*1 - 0 = λ. So we have y_0 = λ. Then y_1 = 2λ - sqrt(2 - λ^2). Then y_2 = 2λ - sqrt(2 - y_1^2), etc. This recurrence describes the gap between λ m and S_{m-1} for odd m. The condition for Alice to win on her turn at m = 2k+1 is that y_k > √2. Because y_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} is the amount she adds on that turn. So if y_k > √2, then she wins. So Alice's strategy is to see if the sequence y_k ever exceeds √2. Bazza's strategy is to try to keep y_k ≤ √2 for all k. So the game reduces to: Given λ > 0, define the recurrence y_{n+1} = 2λ - sqrt(2 - y_n^2), with y_0 = λ. Determine if there exists n such that y_n > √2. If yes, Alice can win by playing the greedy strategy. If not, Bazza can prevent her from ever winning by playing the greedy strategy (i.e., always using the maximum allowed sum increase) and she never gets a gap > √2. But note that if y_k ≤ √2 for all k, then the sequence may converge to a fixed point. Let's analyze this recurrence.

We need to find the set of λ for which the sequence y_n stays ≤ √2 for all n. This is a one-dimensional dynamical system. We can analyze the function f(y) = 2λ - sqrt(2 - y^2). The recurrence is y_{n+1} = f(y_n). The fixed points satisfy y = 2λ - sqrt(2 - y^2). Solve: sqrt(2 - y^2) = 2λ - y => square: 2 - y^2 = (2λ - y)^2 = 4λ^2 - 4λy + y^2 => 2 - y^2 = 4λ^2 - 4λy + y^2 => bring: 2 - y^2 - 4λ^2 + 4λy - y^2 = 0 => 2 - 4λ^2 + 4λy - 2y^2 = 0 => divide 2: 1 - 2λ^2 + 2λy - y^2 = 0 => y^2 - 2λy + (2λ^2 - 1) = 0 => y = λ ± sqrt(λ^2 - (2λ^2 - 1)) = λ ± sqrt(1 - λ^2). So fixed points are y = λ ± √(1 - λ^2). These are real only if λ ≤ 1. So for λ ≤ 1, there are two fixed points: y_- = λ - √(1 - λ^2) and y_+ = λ + √(1 - λ^2). Note that y_+ ≥ 1? Actually for λ=1, y_+ = 1 + 0 = 1. For λ=0.5, y_+ = 0.5 + √(0.75)=0.5+0.866=1.366. y_- = 0.5 - 0.866 = -0.366. So the fixed points are not both in the domain of the recurrence? The function f(y) is defined for y ∈ [-√2, √2] because sqrt(2 - y^2) requires |y| ≤ √2. So the recurrence is only defined for y_n in that interval. The initial y_0 = λ is in that interval if λ ≤ √2, which it is for λ ≤ √2. For λ > √2, the initial y_0 = λ > √2, so y_0 > √2 already, which means Alice wins on her first move (since y_0 = λ*1 - S_0 = λ > √2). So for λ > √2, Alice has an immediate winning strategy. So the interesting region is λ ≤ √2.

For λ ≤ 1, we have fixed points y_+ and y_-. Which one is stable? We can analyze the derivative f'(y) = derivative of 2λ - sqrt(2 - y^2) = - (1/(2√(2 - y^2)) * (-2y)) = y / √(2 - y^2). So f'(y) = y / √(2 - y^2). For y > 0, f'(y) > 0. So f is increasing for y > 0. The derivative at the fixed points: For y_+ = λ + √(1 - λ^2), we have y_+ ≥ 1. Compute y_+ / √(2 - y_+^2). Since y_+^2 = λ^2 + 2λ√(1-λ^2) + (1-λ^2) = 1 + 2λ√(1-λ^2). So 2 - y_+^2 = 2 - (1 + 2λ√(1-λ^2)) = 1 - 2λ√(1-λ^2). This is positive only if λ ≤ 1/√2? Actually for λ=1, y_+=1, 2-1=1, √(1)=1, f'(1)=1/1=1. For λ=0.5, y_+=1.366, y_+^2=1.866, 2-1.866=0.134, √=0.366, f'(1.366)=1.366/0.366≈3.73>1. So the fixed point y_+ is repelling (since f'(y_+) > 1). For y_- = λ - √(1-λ^2), we have y_- ≤ 0. For λ=1, y_-=1-0=1? Actually λ=1, y_- = 1 - 0 = 1, same as y_+? But the formula y_- = λ - √(1-λ^2) gives 1-0=1, so they coincide at λ=1. For λ=0.5, y_- = 0.5 - 0.866 = -0.366, which is negative. The derivative at y_-: y_- is negative, so f'(y_-) = y_- / √(2 - y_-^2) is negative. Its absolute value? For y_- = -0.366, √(2 - 0.134)=√1.866=1.366, f' = -0.366/1.366 ≈ -0.268, which has magnitude < 1. So the fixed point y_- is attracting (since |f'(y_-)| < 1). So the recurrence tends to converge to y_- if it stays within the interval. So the long-term behavior is that y_n → y_- = λ - √(1-λ^2). Note that y_- is nonpositive for λ < 1. At λ=1, y_- = 1. So the sequence y_n starting from y_0 = λ will converge to y_- if the iteration stays in the interval. But we need to see if y_n ever exceeds √2. The initial y_0 = λ, which for λ ≤ √2 is ≤ 1.414. So y_0 ≤ 1.414. For λ close to 1, y_0=1, y_- = λ - √(1-λ^2) = 1 - 0 = 1. So the fixed point is 1. For λ=0.99, y_0=0.99, y_- = 0.99 - √(1-0.9801)=0.99 - √0.0199=0.99 - 0.141=0.849. So y_- = 0.849. So the sequence will converge to 0.849. Since y_0=0.99, which is less than √2=1.414, and the fixed point is 0.849, the sequence will be decreasing? Let's see the recurrence: y_{n+1} = 2λ - sqrt(2 - y_n^2). For λ=0.99, compute y_1 = 2*0.99 - sqrt(2 - 0.99^2) = 1.98 - sqrt(2 - 0.9801) = 1.98 - sqrt(1.0199) = 1.98 - 1.00995 = 0.97005. So y_1 ≈ 0.970, which is less than y_0=0.99. Then y_2 = 1.98 - sqrt(2 - 0.970^2) = 1.98 - sqrt(2 - 0.9409) = 1.98 - sqrt(1.0591) = 1.98 - 1.029 = 0.951. So it decreases further. So it will converge to y_- = 0.849. Since all y_n are positive (starting from 0.99), they are all less than √2. So for λ=0.99, the condition y_n > √2 never occurs, but we previously simulated the greedy strategy and found that Alice won at turn 13? We need to solve the problem: Inekoalaty game. Two players, Alice and Bazza. Positive real number λ known to both. Game proceeds in turns n=1,2,3,... On odd n (Alice's turn), she chooses nonnegative real x_n such that the sum of all chosen numbers up to n is ≤ λ n. On even n (Bazza's turn), he chooses nonnegative real x_n such that the sum of squares up to n is ≤ n. If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the game ends and the other player wins. If it goes on forever, neither wins. Determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy, and all for which Bazza has a winning strategy.

We need to analyze the game. It is a combinatorial game with real numbers. The constraints are cumulative: for Alice's turns, she must keep the total sum S_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i ≤ λ n. For Bazza's turns, he must keep the total sum of squares Q_n = sum_{i=1}^n x_i^2 ≤ n. Both players know all previous numbers.

The game ends when a player cannot choose a number satisfying the constraint (given the previous numbers). The other player wins. So each player wants to force the opponent into a situation where they cannot choose a number. The game can be infinite if both can always choose numbers satisfying constraints.

We need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy (i.e., she can force a win), and for which λ Bazza has a winning strategy.

Interpretation: This is a game of perfect information with real numbers. The players can choose any nonnegative real numbers within constraints. We need to analyze the game in terms of cumulative sums and squares. Let's denote the state after each turn. The game can be seen as: at odd turns, Alice chooses a number that keeps sum ≤ λ n, and she wants to eventually make the sum of squares exceed the next even index. At even turns, Bazza chooses a number that keeps sum of squares ≤ n, and he wants to eventually make the sum exceed λ n for the next odd turn. So we need to consider the "safe" region where both can continue.

- For all even n: Q_{n-1} ≤ n (so Bazza can move)
- For all odd m: S_{m-1} ≤ λ m (so Alice can move)

Also, the constraints on the moves themselves: For Alice, she chooses x_m such that S_m = S_{m-1} + x_m ≤ λ m. So she will choose x_m to try to increase squares. For Bazza, he chooses x_n such that Q_n = Q_{n-1} + x_n^2 ≤ n. So he will choose x_n to try to increase sum (to make Alice lose) while staying within square budget.

Thus the game is about controlling these two resources. It seems plausible that there is a threshold λ0 such that if λ > λ0, Alice can force a win; if λ < λ0, Bazza can force a win; and if λ = λ0, the game is a draw (both can survive indefinitely). We need to find λ0.

To determine if Bazza can force a win for λ=0.9, we need to analyze the game systematically.

Our earlier analysis suggests that for λ > √2, Alice can win immediately. But we saw that for λ=1, she can win on turn 3. So for λ=0.9, we need to see if Alice can still win. The threshold might be λ=1? But we need to test λ=0.9 carefully. Perhaps there is a critical value λ0 = √2/3? But we saw that for λ=0.5 (>√2/3), Bazza can win? Actually we need to determine the winning region for Alice and Bazza. The problem asks: Determine all values of λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all those for which Bazza has a winning strategy. So we need to find the set of λ for which Alice can force a win, and the set for which Bazza can force a win. Possibly there is a range where neither has a winning strategy (draw). Let's analyze the game mathematically.

Define the game state after each turn. The game is about two cumulative constraints. Let's denote the sequences: x_1, x_2, ... nonnegative reals.

Constraints:

For odd n: S_n ≤ λ n.
For even n: Q_n ≤ n.

Game ends when a player cannot make a move. So Alice loses on her turn if S_{n-1} > λ n (since she cannot choose x_n to make S_n ≤ λ n, even x_n=0 gives S_n = S_{n-1} > λ n). Similarly, Bazza loses on his turn if Q_{n-1} > n.

So the game is about controlling the sums.

We can think of the game as: Alice wants to eventually make Q_{odd} > odd+1 (so Bazza loses on next turn). Bazza wants to eventually make S_{even} > λ (even+1) (so Alice loses on her next turn). But note that the constraints are cumulative, so it's about the sequences.

Idea: The game is similar to a "pursuit" where each player tries to push the other's resource over a threshold. There is a known technique: consider the "potential" function F_n = S_n - λ n (for odd n) and G_n = Q_n - n (for even n). The game ends when either potential becomes positive (for the player who is about to move). The players can choose numbers to affect these potentials.

We need to find for which λ there is a strategy for Alice to force that for some odd m, Q_m > m+1, i.e., Bazza cannot move. Conversely, for which λ Bazza can force that for some even n, S_n > λ n+? Actually if Bazza makes S_{even} > λ (even+1), then Alice cannot move on her next turn (since S_{even} > λ (even+1)). But careful: Alice's turn after even n is at odd n+1. She needs S_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+1). She can choose x_{n+1} such that S_{n+1} = S_n + x_{n+1} ≤ λ (n+1). If S_n > λ (n+1), then even with x_{n+1}=0, S_{n+1}=S_n > λ (n+1), so she loses. So condition for Bazza to win is that for some even n, S_n > λ (n+1). Since n is even, n+1 is odd. So Bazza wants to make S_{even} > λ (even+1). Similarly, Alice wants to make Q_{odd} > odd+1.

So we need to analyze the game in terms of these "excess" functions.

Let's define:

For odd m: Let E_m = Q_m - (m+1). Alice wins if she can make E_m > 0 for some odd m.
For even n: Let F_n = S_n - λ (n+1). Bazza wins if he can make F_n > 0 for some even n.

But note that the constraints for moves also involve these potentials. For Alice's turn at odd m, she must ensure that S_m ≤ λ m (so she doesn't lose immediately). She can choose x_m to try to increase Q_m. Similarly, Bazza's turn at even n must ensure Q_n ≤ n, and he can choose x_n to try to increase S_n.

So the game is about two resources: sum S and sum of squares Q. The players have opposite goals.

We can try to find a "safe" region where both can continue indefinitely, and determine the threshold for which one player can force a win.

Observation: The game is symmetric in a sense if we transform variables. Consider the "dual" game: replace λ with 1/λ? Not exactly.

Let's attempt to find a critical λ by considering the "ideal" play where both players try to keep the game going but also try to force a win. Often in such games, there is a threshold λ0 such that if λ > λ0, Alice can win; if λ < λ0, Bazza can win; and at λ = λ0, the game can be infinite (draw). We need to find λ0.

From earlier, we saw that for λ > √2 ≈ 1.4142, Alice can win immediately on her first move by playing x_1 = √2 (which is ≤ λ). But for λ ≤ √2, she cannot win on first move. For λ = 1.4 (<√2), can she win later? Possibly there is a threshold around 1? Let's test λ=1.2. Can Alice win? She wants to eventually make Q_odd > odd+1. Since λ is 1.2, she can choose x_1 up to 1.2. She might try to build up squares. But Bazza can also try to make S_even > λ (even+1). For λ=1.2, λ (even+1) is about 1.2*(2k+1) = 2.4k+1.2. So if Bazza can push S_{2k} above 2.4k+1.2, then Alice loses. This seems plausible.

We need a more systematic approach.

Define the "resource" constraints:

After each odd turn, the sum S_n is constrained: S_n ≤ λ n. After each even turn, the sum of squares Q_n is constrained: Q_n ≤ n.

But note that these constraints are not independent: by Cauchy-Schwarz, S_n^2 ≤ n Q_n. So if Q_n is small, S_n is also small.

We can consider the "maximal" possible S_n given Q_n, and vice versa.

We can try to see if there is a strategy for Bazza to always keep S_n ≤ λ n for all odd n, and also keep Q_n ≤ n for all even n, and prevent Alice from ever making Q_odd > odd+1. That would be a draw. But we need to find λ for which Bazza can force a win.

Let's try to find a strategy for Bazza to make S_n exceed λ (n+1) for some even n.

Bazza can choose x_n on his turns to increase S_n. But he must also respect Q_n ≤ n. So he can allocate square budget to increase sum.

We can think of the game as: Alice chooses x_1, then Bazza chooses x_2, then Alice chooses x_3, etc. They have perfect information.

We can try to compute the "critical" λ by considering the worst-case for Alice: if she wants to avoid losing, she will try to keep S_n as small as possible to prevent Bazza from exceeding λ (n+1). She can also try to build up squares to eventually win.

Alternatively, we can treat the game as a two-player game with a "potential" function that is monotone and see if one player can force a win by using a "strategy stealing" argument.

I think the problem is known: Inekoalaty game. I recall a problem from an IMO Shortlist or something similar: "Alice and Bazza play the inekoalaty game, a two-player game whose rules depend on a positive real number λ. On the nth turn, if n is odd, Alice chooses a nonnegative real x_n such that x_1+...+x_n ≤ λ n. If n is even, Bazza chooses a nonnegative real x_n such that x_1^2+...+x_n^2 ≤ n. If a player cannot choose a suitable x_n, the game ends and the other player wins. Determine all λ for which Alice has a winning strategy and all for which Bazza has a winning strategy." Possibly the answer: Alice wins if λ > 1, Bazza wins if λ < 1, and if λ = 1, the game is a draw (both can survive indefinitely). But we saw that for λ=1, Alice can win on turn 3? Actually for λ=1, she can choose x_1=1, then Q_1=1, then Bazza chooses x_2=0 (since Q_1=1, he cannot exceed 2, he can choose 0). Then Alice's turn n=3: she needs S_3 ≤ 3. She has S_2 = 1. She can choose x_3 up to 2. She wants to make Q_3 > 4. Q_2=1, so she needs x_3^2 > 3, i.e., x_3 > √3 ≈ 1.732. She can choose x_3=1.8, then S_3=2.8 ≤3, Q_3=1+3.24=4.24>4. Then Bazza's turn n=4: Q_3=4.24>4, so he loses because Q_4 ≥ Q_3 >4 and constraint is Q_4 ≤4. So yes, for λ=1, Alice can win. So λ=1 is in Alice's winning region. So the threshold must be less than 1. Let's test λ=0.9: Can Alice win? She can choose x_1=0.9? But then Q_1=0.81. Then Bazza can choose x_2=0.9? He must respect Q_2 ≤2. So we need to see if there is a way for Alice to force a win for λ=0.9.

Let's consider the "resource" trade-off. For a given sum S_n, the minimal sum of squares is achieved by equal numbers. Conversely, for a given sum of squares Q_n, the maximal sum is achieved by concentrating mass. So if Alice wants to increase squares, she can use a large x_m, but that uses sum budget. The sum budget is λ n. So she cannot exceed λ n. So the maximum possible Q_m after her move is when she makes x_m as large as possible, but then the sum might be close to λ m. However, she also has to respect that the sum before her move is S_{m-1}. So she can choose x_m up to λ m - S_{m-1}. Then Q_m = Q_{m-1} + x_m^2. So the maximum possible Q_m given previous state is Q_{m-1} + (λ m - S_{m-1})^2. She wants this to exceed m+1.

Bazza, on his turns, can choose x_n to increase S_n. He can allocate square budget to increase sum. The maximum sum he can achieve given Q_{n-1} is S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}). Because he can choose x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) to maximize sum. So the maximum possible S_n after his move is S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}).

Thus the game can be seen as: at each turn, the player can choose a value that affects the state. The player can choose any value between 0 and the maximum allowed. To force a win, they can try to make the potential exceed the threshold.

We need to find if there is a strategy for Alice to make Q_{odd} > odd+1. Similarly, Bazza wants to make S_{even} > λ (even+1).

Consider the "optimal" play: Alice will try to build up squares, while Bazza will try to build up sum. They have opposite goals. The game is deterministic if both play optimally to win.

We can try to compute the "critical" λ by considering the "worst-case" for Alice: if she tries to maximize Q_m, she must minimize the sum S_{m-1} to have room for x_m. But she can also control previous sums by her own choices. Actually she can choose x_m small to keep S_m low, but that doesn't help squares. She wants to increase squares, so she wants x_m large, but that uses sum budget. So there is a trade-off: she can use a large x_m to increase squares, but then the sum might be high, which could allow Bazza to increase sum on his next turn. However, she can also try to keep the sum low to prevent Bazza from exceeding λ (n+1). So she needs to find a balance.

Perhaps we can treat the game as a "pursuit" where each player tries to push the other's "excess" above a threshold. So for λ=0.9, Bazza can force a win by playing x_2 = 1.3 and then always using the maximum allowed sum increase on his turns. The first move x_1=0.5 worked, but maybe Alice can choose a different x_1 to avoid the loss? In the simulation, we used x_1=0.5 and then on odd turns we used the maximum allowed (x_n = λ n - S_{n-1}) which is the greedy strategy. That led to a win for Bazza. But maybe if she chooses a different x_1, she could still win? Let's analyze the game more generally.

We need to determine for which λ Alice has a winning strategy. The above suggests that for λ=0.9, Bazza wins. For λ=1, Alice wins. So there is a critical λ0 between 0.9 and 1. Possibly λ0 = 1? Let's try to analyze the recurrence more systematically. The greedy strategy for Alice: on odd turns, she plays x_n = λ n - S_{n-1} (the maximum allowed to keep S_n ≤ λ n). This is a natural strategy to maximize her square increase. For Bazza, he plays x_n = sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}) on even turns to maximize sum. This is also natural. So the state evolves deterministically: for n odd, S_n = λ n, and Q_n = Q_{n-1} + (λ n - S_{n-1})^2. For n even, S_n = S_{n-1} + sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}), and Q_n = Q_{n-1} + (sqrt(n - Q_{n-1}))^2 = Q_{n-1} + (n - Q_{n-1}) = n. So after each even turn, Q_n = n exactly. So the sequence of states is: after each even turn, Q_n = n; after each odd turn, S_n = λ n.

So we can compute the state after each pair of turns. Let's define the state at the start of each odd turn (i.e., after an even turn). Let m be odd. After even turn n = m-1, we have Q_{m-1} = m-1 (since m-1 is even). And S_{m-1} is some value. Then Alice's move at m: she chooses x_m = λ m - S_{m-1}, so S_m = λ m. Then Q_m = Q_{m-1} + (λ m - S_{m-1})^2 = (m-1) + (λ m - S_{m-1})^2. Then Bazza's move at m+1 (even): he chooses x_{m+1} = sqrt((m+1) - Q_m) ) = sqrt( (m+1) - (m-1 + (λ m - S_{m-1})^2) ) = sqrt( 2 - (λ m - S_{m-1})^2 ). Then S_{m+1} = S_m + sqrt((m+1) - Q_m) = λ m + sqrt(2 - (λ m - S_{m-1})^2). And Q_{m+1} = m+1 (since after even turn, Q equals the index). So the state at the start of the next odd turn (m+2) is: Q_{m+1} = m+1, and S_{m+1} = λ m + sqrt(2 - (λ m - S_{m-1})^2). So we have a recurrence for S_{odd} values. Let’s denote a_k = S_{2k+1} for k ≥ 0? Actually we need to be consistent: Let m = 2k+1 (odd). Then after even turn 2k, we have Q_{2k} = 2k, and S_{2k} = some value. Then Alice's move at 2k+1: S_{2k+1} = λ(2k+1). Then Q_{2k+1} = 2k + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2. Then Bazza's move at 2k+2: x_{2k+2} = sqrt( (2k+2) - Q_{2k+1} ) = sqrt( (2k+2) - (2k + (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2 ) = sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2. Then S_{2k+2} = S_{2k+1} + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2) = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2). And Q_{2k+2} = 2k+2. So the recurrence for S_{2k} (even) is: S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2). This is a discrete dynamical system. The initial S_0 = 0. Then we compute S_2 = λ*1 + sqrt(2 - (λ*1 - 0)^2) = λ + sqrt(2 - λ^2). Then S_4 = λ*3 + sqrt(2 - (λ*3 - S_2)^2), etc. This recurrence determines the sum after each even turn.

Alice wins if for some odd m, Q_m > m+1, i.e., if (m-1) + (λ m - S_{m-1})^2 > m+1, i.e., (λ m - S_{m-1})^2 > 2, i.e., λ m - S_{m-1} > √2. So the condition for Alice to win on her turn at odd m is that λ m - S_{m-1} > √2. Note that this is the amount she adds to her sum on that turn. If she can make this > √2, then the square sum after her move exceeds m+1, so Bazza loses on the next even turn. So Alice's goal is to eventually have the sum before her turn, S_{m-1}, be such that the gap λ m - S_{m-1} > √2. Since λ m is the allowed sum for her move, she wants S_{m-1} to be sufficiently small relative to λ m.

Bazza's goal is to make S_{m-1} large enough so that λ m - S_{m-1} ≤ √2 for all m (i.e., Alice never gets a gap > √2). But if he can make S_{m-1} > λ m - √2, then the gap is ≤ √2. Actually if S_{m-1} > λ m - √2, then λ m - S_{m-1} < √2, so Alice cannot win on that turn. So Bazza wants to keep S_{m-1} > λ m - √2 for all odd m. Since S_{m-1} is after an even turn, and he controls the even turns, he can try to increase S_{m-1} enough.

Now note that the recurrence for S_{2k} (even) is: S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - S_{2k})^2). Let’s denote g(x) = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - x)^2). Then S_{2k+2} = g(S_{2k}). So we have a sequence starting from S_0 = 0. The fixed point of this recurrence (if it converges) would be a value x such that x = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - (λ(2k+1) - x)^2). But note that the recurrence depends on k, so it's not autonomous. However, we can consider the transformation. Let’s define a new variable: y_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k}. Then the recurrence becomes: S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - y_k^2). Then y_{k+1} = λ(2k+3) - S_{2k+2} = λ(2k+3) - (λ(2k+1) + sqrt(2 - y_k^2)) = 2λ + sqrt(2 - y_k^2) - (something)? Actually λ(2k+3) - λ(2k+1) = 2λ. So y_{k+1} = 2λ - sqrt(2 - y_k^2). So we have a recurrence for y_k: y_{k+1} = 2λ - sqrt(2 - y_k^2). This is an autonomous recurrence! And the initial y_0 = λ*1 - S_0 = λ*1 - 0 = λ. So we have y_0 = λ. Then y_1 = 2λ - sqrt(2 - λ^2). Then y_2 = 2λ - sqrt(2 - y_1^2), etc. This recurrence describes the gap between λ m and S_{m-1} for odd m. The condition for Alice to win on her turn at m = 2k+1 is that y_k > √2. Because y_k = λ(2k+1) - S_{2k} is the amount she adds on that turn. So if y_k > √2, then she wins. So Alice's strategy is to see if the sequence y_k ever exceeds √2. Bazza's strategy is to try to keep y_k ≤ √2 for all k. So the game reduces to: Given λ > 0, define the recurrence y_{n+1} = 2λ - sqrt(2 - y_n^2), with y_0 = λ. Determine if there exists n such that y_n > √2. If yes, Alice can win by playing the greedy strategy. If not, Bazza can prevent her from ever winning by playing the greedy strategy (i.e., always using the maximum allowed sum increase) and she never gets a gap > √2. But note that if y_k ≤ √2 for all k, then the sequence may converge to a fixed point. Let's analyze this recurrence.

We need to find the set of λ for which the sequence y_n stays ≤ √2 for all n. This is a one-dimensional dynamical system. We can analyze the function f(y) = 2λ - sqrt(2 - y^2). The recurrence is y_{n+1} = f(y_n). The fixed points satisfy y = 2λ - sqrt(2 - y^2). Solve: sqrt(2 - y^2) = 2λ - y => square: 2 - y^2 = (2λ - y)^2 = 4λ^2 - 4λy + y^2 => 2 - y^2 = 4λ^2 - 4λy + y^2 => bring: 2 - y^2 - 4λ^2 + 4λy - y^2 = 0 => 2 - 4λ^2 + 4λy - 2y^2 = 0 => divide 2: 1 - 2λ^2 + 2λy - y^2 = 0 => y^2 - 2λy + (2λ^2 - 1) = 0 => y = λ ± sqrt(λ^2 - (2λ^2 - 1)) = λ ± sqrt(1 - λ^2). So fixed points are y = λ ± √(1 - λ^2). These are real only if λ ≤ 1. So for λ ≤ 1, there are two fixed points: y_- = λ - √(1 - λ^2) and y_+ = λ + √(1 - λ^2). Note that y_+ ≥ 1? Actually for λ=1, y_+ = 1 + 0 = 1. For λ=0.5, y_+ = 0.5 + √(0.75)=0.5+0.866=1.366. y_- = 0.5 - 0.866 = -0.366. So the fixed points are not both in the domain of the recurrence? The function f(y) is defined for y ∈ [-√2, √2] because sqrt(2 - y^2) requires |y| ≤ √2. So the recurrence is only defined for y_n in that interval. The initial y_0 = λ is in that interval if λ ≤ √2, which it is for λ ≤ √2. For λ > √2, the initial y_0 = λ > √2, so y_0 > √2 already, which means Alice wins on her first move (since y_0 = λ*1 - S_0 = λ > √2). So for λ > √2, Alice has an immediate winning strategy. So the interesting region is λ ≤ √2.

For λ ≤ 1, we have fixed points y_+ and y_-. Which one is stable? We can analyze the derivative f'(y) = derivative of 2λ - sqrt(2 - y^2) = - (1/(2√(2 - y^2)) * (-2y)) = y / √(2 - y^2). So f'(y) = y / √(2 - y^2). For y > 0, f'(y) > 0. So f is increasing for y > 0. The derivative at the fixed points: For y_+ = λ + √(1 - λ^2), we have y_+ ≥ 1. Compute y_+ / √(2 - y_+^2). Since y_+^2 = λ^2 + 2λ√(1-λ^2) + (1-λ^2) = 1 + 2λ√(1-λ^2). So 2 - y_+^2 = 2 - (1 + 2λ√(1-λ^2)) = 1 - 2λ√(1-λ^2). This is positive only if λ ≤ 1/√2? Actually for λ=1, y_+=1, 2-1=1, √(1)=1, f'(1)=1/1=1. For λ=0.5, y_+=1.366, y_+^2=1.866, 2-1.866=0.134, √=0.366, f'(1.366)=1.366/0.366≈3.73>1. So the fixed point y_+ is repelling (since f'(y_+) > 1). For y_- = λ - √(1-λ^2), we have y_- ≤ 0. For λ=1, y_-=1-0=1? Actually λ=1, y_- = 1 - 0 = 1, same as y_+? But the formula y_- = λ - √(1-λ^2) gives 1-0=1, so they coincide at λ=1. For λ=0.5, y_- = 0.5 - 0.866 = -0.366, which is negative. The derivative at y_-: y_- is negative, so f'(y_-) = y_- / √(2 - y_-^2) is negative. Its absolute value? For y_- = -0.366, √(2 - 0.134)=√1.866=1.366, f' = -0.366/1.366 ≈ -0.268, which has magnitude < 1. So the fixed point y_- is attracting (since |f'(y_-)| < 1). So the recurrence tends to converge to y_- if it stays within the interval. So the long-term behavior is that y_n → y_- = λ - √(1-λ^2). Note that y_- is nonpositive for λ < 1. At λ=1, y_- = 1. So the sequence y_n starting from y_0 = λ will converge to y_- if the iteration stays in the interval. But we need to see if y_n ever exceeds √2. The initial y_0 = λ, which for λ ≤ √2 is ≤ 1.414. But if she plays a smaller amount, y_{k+1} can be different. So we need to analyze the game as a two-player game with the ability to choose numbers.

Let's define the state after each even turn as (S, Q) with Q = index. The players have the constraints. The game ends when a player cannot move. The player who is about to move loses if the constraint cannot be satisfied. So Alice loses if S > λ n (where n is odd), and Bazza loses if Q > n (where n is even). So the game is about controlling S and Q.

We can consider the "critical" value of λ at which the game is a draw (both can survive indefinitely). This is a known game: Inekoalaty. I suspect the answer is: Alice has a winning strategy if and only if λ > 1, and Bazza has a winning strategy if and only if λ < 1, and if λ = 1, the game is a draw (both can survive indefinitely). But our simulations contradict that: For λ=0.99, we found a strategy for Alice that wins (by playing x_1=0.5). So maybe λ=0.99 is in Alice's winning region.
